<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Human overpopulation</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The condition where human numbers exceed the short or long-term carrying capacity of the environment</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Overpopulate" and "Overpopulating" redirect here. For the general concept of overpopulation, see <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">Overpopulation</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_curve.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="547" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/350px-Population_curve.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/525px-Population_curve.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/700px-Population_curve.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_curve.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph of human population from 10000 BCE to 2000 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>. It shows exponential rise in world population that has taken place since the eighteenth century.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="481" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/220px-World_population_v3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/330px-World_population_v3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/440px-World_population_v3.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World population v3</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_2017_test.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Population_2017_test.png/500px-Population_2017_test.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Population_2017_test.png/750px-Population_2017_test.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Population_2017_test.png/1000px-Population_2017_test.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_2017_test.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of population density by country, per square kilometer. (See <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population_density" title="List of countries by population density">List of countries by population density</a>.</i>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_density.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="4320" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Population_density.png/350px-Population_density.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Population_density.png/525px-Population_density.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Population_density.png/700px-Population_density.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_density.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Areas of high population densities, calculated in 1994</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/350px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/525px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/700px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries and territories by <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">fertility rate</a> (See <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">List of countries and territories by fertility rate</a>.</i>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg/350px-Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg/525px-Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg/700px-Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_growth_rate_world_2013.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human population growth rate in percent, with the variables of births, deaths, immigration, and emigration – 2013</div></div></div>
<p><b>Human overpopulation</b> (or <b>population overshoot</b>) occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of a human population in a specific geographical location exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> of the place occupied by that group. <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">Overpopulation</a> can further be viewed, in a long term perspective, as existing if a population cannot be maintained given the rapid depletion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resources" title="Non-renewable resources">non-renewable resources</a> or given the degradation of the capacity of the environment to give support to the population. Changes in lifestyle could reverse overpopulated status without a large population reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>human overpopulation</i> refers to the relationship between the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">entire human population</a> and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>: the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> or to smaller geographical areas such as countries. Overpopulation can result from an increase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">births</a>, a decline in <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rates</a>, an increase in <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">unsustainable</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depletion_of_resources" title="Depletion of resources">depletion of resources</a>. It is possible for very sparsely populated areas to be overpopulated if the area has a meagre or non-existent capability to sustain life (e.g. a <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>). Advocates of population moderation cite issues like <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a>, and risk of <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> as a basis to argue for <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a>. Scientists suggest that the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a> as a result of overpopulation, <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">profligate consumption</a> and proliferation of technology has pushed the planet into a new geological <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epoch</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_population" title="Human population">Human population</a> has been rising continuously since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, around the year 1350,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> although the most significant increase has been since the 1950s, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">medical advancements</a> and increases in <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a>. The rate of population growth has been declining since the 1980s, while the absolute total numbers are increasing. Recent rate increases in several countries previously enjoying steady declines are also apparently contributing to continued growth in total numbers. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> has expressed concerns on continued population growth in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-9">[9]</a></sup> Recent research has demonstrated that those concerns are well grounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (May 2018)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> As of October 30, 2018 the world's human population is estimated to be 7.661 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Or, 7,622,106,064 on May 14, 2018 and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> calculates 7,472,985,269 for that same date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCBcite_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCBcite-12">[12]</a></sup> and over 7 billion by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN7bn_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN7bn-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Most contemporary estimates for the carrying capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> under existing conditions are between 4 billion and 16 billion. Depending on which estimate is used, human overpopulation may or may not have already occurred. Nevertheless, the rapid recent increase in human population is causing some concern. The population is <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">expected</a> to reach between 8 and 10.5 billion between the years 2040<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-18">[18]</a></sup> In 2017, the United Nations increased the medium variant projections to 9.8 billion for 2050 and 11.2 billion for 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The recent rapid increase in human population over the past three centuries has raised concerns that the planet may not be able to sustain present or future numbers of inhabitants. The <a href="/wiki/IAP_statement_on_population_growth" title="IAP statement on population growth">InterAcademy Panel Statement on Population Growth</a>, circa 1994, stated that many environmental problems, such as rising levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, are aggravated by the population expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Other problems associated with overpopulation include the increased demand for resources such as fresh water and food, <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, consumption of natural resources (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>) faster than the rate of regeneration, and a deterioration in living conditions. Wealthy but highly populated territories like <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> rely on food imports from overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This was severely felt during the <a href="/wiki/World_war#The_World_Wars" title="World war">World Wars</a> when, despite food efficiency initiatives like "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dig_for_victory#World_War_II" title="Dig for victory">dig for victory</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">food rationing</a>, Britain needed to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">fight to secure import routes</a>. However, many believe that waste and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Over-consumption" title="Over-consumption">over-consumption</a>, especially by wealthy nations, is putting more strain on the environment than overpopulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>In spite of  concerns about overpopulation, widespread in developed countries, the number of people living in extreme poverty globally shows a stable decline (this has been disputed by some experts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>), even though the population has grown seven-fold over the last 200 years. Child mortality has declined, which in turn has led to reduced birth rates, thus slowing overall population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup> The global number of famine-related deaths have declined, and food supply per person has increased with population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most countries have no direct policy of limiting their birth rates, but the rates have still fallen due to education about family planning and increasing access to birth control and contraception.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_concern">History of concern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of concern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City,_Vietnam.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/275px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/413px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/550px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City,_Vietnam.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thousands of scooters make their way through <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Ho Chi Minh City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Concern about overpopulation is an ancient topic. <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> was a resident of the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> in the second century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, when the population of the world was about 190 million (only 3–4% of what it is today). He notably said: "What most frequently meets our view (and occasions complaint) is our teeming population. Our numbers are burdensome to the world, which can hardly support us.... In very deed, pestilence, and famine, and wars, and earthquakes have to be regarded as a remedy for nations, as the means of pruning the luxuriance of the human race." Before that, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and others broached the topic as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout recorded history, population growth has usually been slow despite high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_rates" title="Birth rates">birth rates</a>, due to war, <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plagues</a> and other diseases, and high <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a>. During the 750 years before the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the world's population increased very slowly, remaining under 250 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the beginning of the 19th century, the world population had grown to a billion individuals, and intellectuals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> predicted that humankind would outgrow its available resources, because a finite amount of land would be incapable of supporting a population with a limitless potential for increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Mercantillists</a> argued that a large population was a form of wealth, which made it possible to create bigger markets and armies.
</p><p>During the 19th century, Malthus's work was often interpreted in a way that blamed the poor alone for their condition and helping them was said to worsen conditions in the long run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claeys_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-31">[31]</a></sup> This resulted, for example, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_poor_laws" title="English poor laws">English poor laws</a> of 1834<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claeys_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-31">[31]</a></sup> and in a hesitating response to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish Great Famine</a> of 1845–52.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The UN publication 'World population prospects' (2017) projects that the world population will reach 9.8 billion in 2050 and 11.2 billion in 2100. Human population is predicted to stabilise soon thereafter.  
</p><p>A 2014 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> challenges this projection, asserting that population growth will continue into the next century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Adrian Raftery, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> professor of statistics and sociology and one of the contributors to the study, says: "The consensus over the past 20 years or so was that world population, which is currently around 7 billion, would go up to 9 billion and level off or probably decline. We found there's a 70 percent probability the world population will not stabilize this century. Population, which had sort of fallen off the world's agenda, remains a very important issue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> A more recent UN projection suggests the population could grow to as many as 15 billion by 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15billion_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-15billion-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, more than a third of 50 Nobel prize-winning scientists surveyed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education" title="Times Higher Education">Times Higher Education</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Lindau_Nobel_Laureate_Meetings" title="Lindau Nobel Laureate Meetings">Lindau Nobel Laureate Meetings</a> said that human overpopulation and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> are the two greatest threats facing humankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In November that same year, a <a href="/wiki/World_Scientists%27_Warning_to_Humanity" title="World Scientists' Warning to Humanity">statement</a> by 15,364 scientists from 184 countries indicated that rapid human population growth is the "primary driver behind many ecological and even societal threats."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_population">Human population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Human population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_population_growth">History of population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History of population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">World population</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="599" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/225px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/338px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/450px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Data from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Population" title="World Population">World Population</a>.</div></div></div>

<p>The human population has gone through a number of periods of growth since the dawn of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> period, around 10,000 BCE. The beginning of civilization roughly coincides with the receding of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_ice" title="Glacial ice">glacial ice</a> following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
It is estimated that between 1–5 million people, subsisting on hunting and <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foraging</a>, inhabited the Earth in the period before the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>, when human activity shifted away from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter_gatherer#History" title="Hunter gatherer">hunter-gathering</a> and towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oasis_Theory" title="Oasis Theory">very primitive farming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 8000 BCE, at the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">dawn of agriculture</a>, the population of the world was approximately 5 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldometer_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldometer-42">[42]</a></sup> The next several millennia saw a steady increase in the population, with very rapid growth beginning in 1000 BCE, and a peak of between 200 and 300 million people in 1 BCE.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> caused <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Europe's population</a> to drop by around 50% between 541 and the 8th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Steady growth resumed in 800 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> However, growth was again disrupted by frequent <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plagues</a>; most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> during the 14th century. The effects of the Black Death are thought to have reduced the world's population, then at an estimated 450 million, to between 350 and 375 million by 1400.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The population of Europe stood at over 70 million in 1340;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> these levels did not return until 200 years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> England's population reached an estimated 5.6 million in 1650, up from an estimated 2.6 million in 1500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> New crops from the Americas via the Spanish colonizers in the 16th century contributed to the population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other parts of the globe, China's population at the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> in 1368 stood close to 60 million, approaching 150 million by the end of the dynasty in 1644.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The population of the Americas in 1500 may have been between 50 and 100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Encounters between European explorers and populations in the rest of the world often introduced local <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a> of extraordinary virulence. Archaeological evidence indicates that the death of around 90% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_history_of_American_indigenous_peoples" title="Population history of American indigenous peoples">Native American population</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> was caused by <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, measles, and influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Europeans introduced diseases alien to the indigenous people, therefore they did not have immunity to these foreign diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, during the 18th century, the rate of population growth began to increase. By the end of the century, the world's population was estimated at just under 1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-censushistorical_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-censushistorical-55">[55]</a></sup> At the turn of the 20th century, the world's population was roughly 1.6 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-censushistorical_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-censushistorical-55">[55]</a></sup> By 1940, this figure had increased to 2.3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Each subsequent addition of a billion humans took less and less time: 33 years to reach three billion in 1960, 14 years for four billion in 1974, 13 years for five billion in 1987, and 12 years for six billion in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dramatic growth beginning in 1950 (above 1.8% per year) coincided with greatly increased food production as a result of the industrialization of agriculture brought about by the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution-58">[58]</a></sup> The rate of human population growth peaked in 1964, at about 2.1% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s population grew from 97 million in 1961 to 237.6 million in 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> a 145% increase in 49 years. In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the population grew from 361.1 million people in 1951 to just over 1.2 billion by 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> a 235% increase in 60 years.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">

<p>There is concern over the sharp population increase in many countries, especially in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, that has occurred over the last several decades, and that it is creating problems with land management, natural resources and access to water supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The population of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> has, for example, grown from 6,279,921 in 1993 to 10,329,208 in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">DRC</a> are witnessing a similar growth in population. The situation is most acute in western, central and eastern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-10">[10]</a></sup> Refugees from places like <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> have further strained the resources of neighboring states like Chad and Egypt. Chad is also host to roughly 255,000 <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> from Sudan's <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> region, and about 77,000 refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, while approximately 188,000 Chadians have been displaced by their own civil war and famines, have either fled to either the Sudan, the Niger or, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to UN data, there are on average 250 babies born each minute, or more than 130 million a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections_of_population_growth">Projections of population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Projections of population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Projections_of_population_growth" title="Projections of population growth">Projections of population growth</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Continent</th>
<th>Projected 2050 population<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Africa</td>
<td>1.8 billion
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Asia</td>
<td>5.3 billion
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Europe</td>
<td>628 million
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Latin America and Caribbean</td>
<td>809 million
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>North America</td>
<td>392 million
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>According to projections, the world population will continue to grow until at least 2050, with the population reaching 9 billion in 2040,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/World_population#Forecasts" title="World population">predictions</a> putting the population as high as 11 billion in 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The median estimate for future growth sees the world population reaching 8.6 billion in 2030, 9.8 billion in 2050 and 11.2 billion by 2100<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-73">[73]</a></sup> assuming a continuing decrease in average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> from 2.5 births per woman in 2010–2015 to 2.2 in 2045–2050 and to 2.0 in 2095–2100, according to the medium-variant projection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-73">[73]</a></sup>.  <a href="/wiki/Walter_Greiling" title="Walter Greiling">Walter Greiling</a> projected in the 1950s that world population would reach a peak of about nine billion, in the 21st century, and then stop growing, after a readjustment of the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> and a sanitation of the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> estimated that the world's population was growing at the rate of 1.14% (or about 75 million people) per year and according to data from the CIA's <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">World Factbook</a>, the world human population currently increases by 145 every minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the United Nations' World Population Prospects report:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="863" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg/400px-Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg/600px-Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg/800px-Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Net_Population_Growth_in_2016.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Net annual human population increase by country – 2016.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> is currently growing by approximately 74 million people per year. Current United Nations predictions estimate that the world population will reach 9.0 billion around 2050, assuming a decrease in average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> from 2.5 down to 2.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNPop_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNPop-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>Almost all growth will take place in the less developed regions, where today's 5.3 billion population of underdeveloped countries is expected to increase to 7.8 billion in 2050. By contrast, the population of the more developed regions will remain mostly unchanged, at 1.2 billion. An exception is the United States population, which is expected to increase by 44% from 2008 to 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2000–2005, the average world fertility was 2.65 children per woman, about half the level in 1950–1955 (5 children per woman). In the medium variant, global fertility is projected to decline further to 2.05 children per woman.</li>
<li>During 2005–2050, nine countries are expected to account for half of the world's projected population increase: <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, United States, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, and China, listed according to the size of their contribution to population growth. China would be higher still in this list were it not for its <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a>.</li>
<li>Global life expectancy at birth is expected to continue rising from 65 years in 2000–2005 to 75 years in 2045–2050. In the more developed regions, the projection is to 82 years by 2050. Among the least developed countries, where life expectancy today is just under 50 years, it is expected to increase to 66 years by 2045–2050.</li>
<li>The population of 51 countries or areas is expected to be lower in 2050 than in 2005.</li>
<li>During 2005–2050, the net number of international migrants to more developed regions is projected to be 98 million. Because deaths are projected to exceed births in the more developed regions by 73 million during 2005–2050, population growth in those regions will largely be due to international migration.</li>
<li>In 2000–2005, net migration in 28 countries either prevented <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a> or doubled at least the contribution of natural increase (births minus deaths) to population growth.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">Birth rates</a> are now falling in a small percentage of <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, while the actual populations in many <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a> would fall without immigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNPop_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNPop-77">[77]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_growth">Urban growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Urban growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/350px-2006megacities.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/525px-2006megacities.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/700px-2006megacities.PNG 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Urban areas with at least one million inhabitants in 2006. 3% of the world's population lived in cities in 1800, rising to 47% at the end of the twentieth century.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1800 only 3% of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world's population</a> lived in cities. By the 20th century's close, 47% did so. In 1950 there were 83 cities with populations exceeding one million; but by 2007 this had risen to 468 agglomerations of more than one million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> If the trend continues, the world's <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">urban population</a> will double every 38 years, according to researchers. The UN forecasts that today's urban population of 3.2 billion will rise to nearly 5 billion by 2030, when three out of five people will live in cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The increase will be most dramatic in the poorest and least-urbanised continents, Asia and Africa. Projections indicate that most urban growth over the next 25 years will be in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> One billion people, one-seventh of the world's population, or one-third of urban population, now live in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanty_towns" title="Shanty towns">shanty towns</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> which are seen as "breeding grounds" for social problems such as unemployment, poverty, crime, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" title="Drug addiction">drug addiction</a>, alcoholism, and other social ills. In many poor countries, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slums" title="Slums">slums</a> exhibit high rates of disease due to unsanitary conditions, malnutrition, and lack of basic health care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, there were 18 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megacities" title="Megacities">megacities</a> – <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbations</a> such as Tokyo, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, Mexico City, <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, São Paulo, London and New York City – that have populations in excess of 10 million inhabitants. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Tokyo" title="Greater Tokyo">Greater Tokyo</a> already has 35 million, more than the entire population of Canada (at 34.1 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Far Eastern Economic Review</i>, Asia alone will have at least 10 'hypercities' by 2025, that is, cities inhabited by more than 19 million people, including <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a> (24.9 million people), <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> (25 million), <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> (26.5 million), <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> (27 million) and <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> (33 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a> has grown from 300,000 in 1950 to an estimated 15 million today, and the Nigerian government estimates that city will have expanded to 25 million residents by 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Chinese experts forecast that Chinese cities will contain 800 million people by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kathmandu_street.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/220px-Kathmandu_street.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/330px-Kathmandu_street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/440px-Kathmandu_street.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kathmandu_street.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Street in <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a></div></div></div>
<p>From a historical perspective, <a href="/wiki/Technological_revolution" title="Technological revolution">technological revolutions</a> have coincided with population expansion. There have been three major technological revolutions – the tool-making revolution, the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">agricultural revolution</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> – all of which allowed humans more access to food, resulting in subsequent population explosions. For example, the use of tools, such as bow and arrow, allowed primitive hunters greater access to more high energy foods (e.g. animal meat). Similarly, the transition to farming about 10,000 years ago greatly increased the overall food supply, which was used to support more people. Food production further increased with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> as machinery, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> were used to increase land under cultivation as well as crop yields. Today, starvation is caused by economic and political forces rather than a lack of the means to produce food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant increases in human population occur whenever the <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a> exceeds the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_rate" title="Death rate">death rate</a> for extended periods of time. Traditionally, the fertility rate is strongly influenced by cultural and social norms that are rather stable and therefore slow to adapt to changes in the social, technological, or environmental conditions. For example, when death rates fell during the 19th and 20th century – as a result of improved sanitation, child immunizations, and other advances in medicine – allowing more newborns to survive, the fertility rate did not adjust downward, resulting in significant population growth. Until the 1700s, seven out of ten children died before reaching reproductive age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Today, more than nine out of ten children born in industrialized nations reach adulthood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There is a strong correlation between overpopulation and poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In contrast, the invention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_control_pill" title="Birth control pill">birth control pill</a> and other modern methods of contraception resulted in a dramatic decline in the number of children per household in all but the very poorest countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> has sustained human population growth. This dates back to prehistoric times, when agricultural methods were first developed, and continues to the present day, with fertilizers, agrochemicals, large-scale mechanization, genetic manipulation, and other technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans have historically <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploited the environment</a> using the easiest, most accessible resources first. The richest farmland was plowed and the richest mineral ore mined first. Ceballos, Ehrlich A and Ehrlich P said that overpopulation is demanding the use of ever more creative, expensive and/or environmentally destructive means in order to exploit ever more difficult to access and/or poorer quality natural resources to satisfy consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_transition">Demographic transition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demographic transition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">Demographic transition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">Sub-replacement fertility</a></div>
<p>The theory of demographic transition held that, after the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> increase, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_size" title="Family size">family sizes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rates</a> decline. However, as new data has become available, it has been observed that after a certain level of development (<a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">HDI</a> equal to 0.86 or higher) the fertility increases again and is often represented as a "J" shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This means that both the worry that the theory generated about aging populations and the complacency it bred regarding the future environmental impact of population growth could need reevaluation.
</p><p>Factors cited in the old theory included such social factors as later ages of marriage, the growing desire of many women in such settings to seek careers outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_rearing" title="Child rearing">child rearing</a> and domestic work, and the decreased need for children in industrialized settings. The latter factor stems from the fact that children <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">perform a great deal of work</a> in small-scale agricultural societies, and work less in industrial ones; it has been cited to explain the decline in birth rates in industrializing regions.
</p><p>Many countries have high population growth rates but lower <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rates</a> because high population growth in the past skewed the age demographic toward a young age, so the population still rises as the more numerous younger generation approaches maturity.
"Demographic entrapment" is a concept developed by Maurice King, Honorary Research Fellow at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leeds" title="University of Leeds">University of Leeds</a>, who posits that this phenomenon occurs when a country has a population larger than its carrying capacity, no possibility of migration, and exports too little to be able to import food. This will cause starvation. He claims that for example many sub-Saharan nations are or will become stuck in demographic entrapment, instead of having a demographic transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the world as a whole, the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">children born per woman</a> decreased from 5.02 to 2.65 between 1950 and 2005. A breakdown by region is as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>Europe – 2.66 to 1.41</li>
<li>North America – 3.47 to 1.99</li>
<li>Oceania – 3.87 to 2.30</li>
<li>Central America – 6.38 to 2.66</li>
<li>South America – 5.75 to 2.49</li>
<li>Asia (excluding Middle East) – 5.85 to 2.43</li>
<li>Middle East &amp; North Africa – 6.99 to 3.37</li>
<li>Sub-Saharan Africa – 6.7 to 5.53</li></ul>
<p>Excluding the theoretical reversal in fertility decrease for high development, the projected world number of children born per woman for 2050 would be around 2.05. Only the Middle East &amp; North Africa (2.09) and Sub-Saharan Africa (2.61) would then have numbers greater than 2.05.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Carrying_capacity">Carrying capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Carrying capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">Carrying capacity</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_contradict.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="620" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ambox_contradict.svg/40px-Ambox_contradict.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ambox_contradict.svg/60px-Ambox_contradict.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ambox_contradict.svg/80px-Ambox_contradict.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>appears to contradict the article <i><a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">Carrying capacity</a></i></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please see discussion on the linked <a href="/wiki/Talk:Carrying_capacity#Where_is_the_contradiction.3F" title="Talk:Carrying capacity">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until the contradictions are resolved.</span> <small><i>(August 2017)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2276" data-file-width="2780" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/220px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/330px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/440px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> placard in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. It shows some negative effects of having too many children.</div></div></div>
<p>Some groups (for example, the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Global_Footprint_Network" title="Global Footprint Network">Global Footprint Network</a>) have stated that the carrying capacity for the human population has been exceeded as measured using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_Footprint" title="Ecological Footprint">Ecological Footprint</a>. In 2006, <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">WWF</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Living_Planet_Report" title="Living Planet Report">Living Planet Report</a>" stated that in order for all humans to live with the current consumption patterns of Europeans, we would be spending three times more than what the planet can renew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Humanity as a whole was using, by 2006, 40 percent more than what Earth can regenerate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>  However, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Martin_(diplomat)" title="Roger Martin (diplomat)">Roger Martin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Population_Matters" title="Population Matters">Population Matters</a> states the view: "the poor want to get rich, and I want them to get rich," with a later addition, "of course we have to change consumption habits,... but we've also got to stabilise our numbers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Another study by the World Wildlife Fund in 2014 found that it would take the equivalent of 1.5 Earths of biocapacity to meet humanity's current levels of consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>But critics question the simplifications and statistical methods used in calculating Ecological Footprints. Therefore, Global Footprint Network and its partner organizations have engaged with national governments and international agencies to test the results – reviews have been produced by France, Germany, the European Commission, Switzerland, Luxembourg, Japan and the United Arab Emirates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Some point out that a more refined method of assessing Ecological Footprint is to designate sustainable versus non-sustainable categories of consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However, if yield estimates were adjusted for sustainable levels of production, the yield figures would be lower, and hence the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overshoot_(ecology)" title="Overshoot (ecology)">overshoot</a> estimated by the Ecological Footprint method even higher.
</p><p>Other studies give particular attention to <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource depletion</a> and increased world affluence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (March 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 1994 study titled <i>Food, Land, Population and the U.S. Economy</i>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=David_Pimentel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="David Pimentel (page does not exist)">David Pimentel</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Giampietro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mario Giampietro (page does not exist)">Mario Giampietro</a> estimated the maximum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._population" title="U.S. population">U.S. population</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable economy</a> at 200 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> And in order to achieve a sustainable economy and avert disaster, the United States would have to reduce its population by at least one-third, and <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> would have to be reduced by two-thirds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many quantitative studies have estimated the world's carrying capacity for humans, that is, a limit to the world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> A meta-analysis of 69 such studies suggests a point estimate of the limit to be 7.7 billion people, while lower and upper meta-bounds for current technology are estimated as 0.65 and 98 billion people, respectively. They conclude: "recent predictions of stabilized world population levels for 2050 exceed several of our meta-estimates of a world population limit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Den_BerghRietveld2004_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Den_BerghRietveld2004-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_human_overpopulation">Effects of human overpopulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Effects of human overpopulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">Overconsumption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Human impact on the environment</a></div>
<p>Some more problems associated with or exacerbated by human overpopulation and over-consumption are:
</p>
<ul><li>Inadequate fresh water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shiklomanov-11-32_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shiklomanov-11-32-115">[115]</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effluent" title="Effluent">effluent</a> discharge. Some countries, like <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, use energy-expensive <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a> to solve the problem of water shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depletion_of_natural_resources" title="Depletion of natural resources">Depletion of natural resources</a>, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption,_1970-2025,_EIA.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="389" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png/220px-World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png/330px-World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption,_1970-2025,_EIA.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World energy consumption and predictions, 1970–2025.</div></div></div></li>
<li>Increased levels of air pollution, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">noise pollution</a>.</li>
<li>Changes in atmospheric composition and consequent <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></li>
<li>Loss of <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> and increase in <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Deforestation and desertification can be reversed by adopting property rights, and this policy is successful even while the human population continues to grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Mass species extinctions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">contracting biodiversity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> from reduced habitat in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> techniques that sometimes are practiced by <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivators</a>, especially in countries with rapidly expanding rural populations; present <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> rates may be as high as 140,000 <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> lost per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimm_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm-129">[129]</a></sup> As of February 2011, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> lists a total of 801 animal species having gone extinct during recorded human history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> although the vast majority of extinctions are thought to be undocumented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimm_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm-129">[129]</a></sup> Biodiversity would continue to grow at an exponential rate if not for human influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_King_(scientist)" title="David King (scientist)">David King</a>, former chief scientific adviser to the UK government, told a parliamentary inquiry: "It is self-evident that the massive growth in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_population" title="Human population">human population</a> through the 20th century has had more impact on biodiversity than any other single factor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul and Anne Ehrlich</a> said population growth is one of the main drivers of the Earth's extinction crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_dolphin" title="Yangtze River dolphin">Yangtze River dolphin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_gray_whale" title="Atlantic gray whale">Atlantic gray whale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_black_rhinoceros" title="Western black rhinoceros">West African black rhino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merriam%27s_elk" title="Merriam's elk">Merriam's elk</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_grizzly_bear" title="California grizzly bear">California grizzly bear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_trout" title="Silver trout">silver trout</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_pike" title="Blue pike">blue pike</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dusky_seaside_sparrow" title="Dusky seaside sparrow">dusky seaside sparrow</a> are all victims of human overpopulation.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Chris_Hedges" title="Chris Hedges">Chris Hedges</a>, 2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<ul><li>High infant and child mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> High rates of infant mortality are associated with poverty. Rich countries with high population densities have low rates of infant mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> However, both global poverty and infant mortality has declined over the last 200 years of population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li>Intensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factory_farming" title="Factory farming">factory farming</a> to support large populations. It results in human threats including the evolution and spread of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistant_bacteria" title="Antibiotic resistant bacteria">antibiotic resistant bacteria</a> diseases, excessive air and water pollution, and new viruses that infect humans.</li>
<li>Increased chance of the emergence of new <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> For many environmental and social reasons, including overcrowded living conditions, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> and inadequate, inaccessible, or non-existent health care, the poor are more likely to be exposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease#Mortality_from_infectious_diseases" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">Starvation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO-Italy_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-Italy-140">[140]</a></sup> or poor diet with ill health and diet-deficiency diseases (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rickets" title="Rickets">rickets</a>). However, rich countries with high population densities do not have famine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-w1_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-w1-141">[141]</a></sup></li>
<li>Poverty coupled with inflation in some regions and a resulting low level of capital formation. Poverty and inflation are aggravated by bad government and bad economic policies. Many countries with high population densities have eliminated absolute poverty and keep their inflation rates very low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage-Index-Economic_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-heritage-Index-Economic-142">[142]</a></sup></li>
<li>Low <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> in countries with fastest growing populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Overall life expectancy has increased globally despite of population growth, including countries with fast-growing populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>Unhygienic living conditions for many based upon water resource depletion, discharge of raw sewage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and solid waste disposal. However, this problem can be reduced with the adoption of sewers. For example, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karachi,_Pakistan" title="Karachi, Pakistan">Karachi, Pakistan</a> installed sewers, its infant mortality rate fell substantially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>Elevated crime rate due to drug cartels and increased theft by people stealing resources to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li>Conflict over scarce resources and crowding, leading to increased levels of warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li>Less personal freedom and more restrictive laws. Laws regulate and shape politics, economics, history and society and serve as a mediator of relations and interactions between people. The higher the population density, the more frequent such interactions become, and thus there develops a need for more laws and/or more restrictive laws to regulate these interactions and relations.
