<td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; line-height:normal; text-align: right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Derrick_Jensen" title="Derrick Jensen">Derrick Jensen</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Endgame_(Derrick_Jensen_books)" title="Endgame (Derrick Jensen books)">Endgame</a></i>, 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Several solutions and mitigation measures have the potential to reduce overpopulation. Some solutions are to be applied on a global planetary level (e.g., via <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> resolutions), while some on a country or state government organization level, and some on a family or an individual level. Some of the proposed mitigations aim to help implement new social, cultural, behavioral and political norms to replace or significantly modify current norms.
</p><p>For example, in societies like China, the government has put policies in place that regulate the number of children allowed to a couple. Other societies have implemented social marketing strategies in order to educate the public on overpopulation effects. "The intervention can be widespread and done at a low cost. A variety of print materials (flyers, brochures, fact sheets, stickers) needs to be produced and distributed throughout the communities such as at local places of worship, sporting events, local food markets, schools and at car parks (taxis / bus stands)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such prompts work to introduce the problem so that new or modified social norms are easier to implement. Certain government policies are making it easier and more socially acceptable to use contraception and abortion methods. An example of a country whose laws and norms are hindering the global effort to slow population growth is Afghanistan. "The approval by Afghan President Hamid Karzai of the Shia Personal Status Law in March 2009 effectively destroyed Shia women's rights and freedoms in Afghanistan. Under this law, women have no right to deny their husbands sex unless they are ill, and can be denied food if they do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists and technologists including e.g. Huesemann, Huesemann, Ehrlich and Ehrlich caution that science and technology, as currently practiced, cannot solve the serious problems global human society faces, and that a cultural-social-political shift is needed to reorient science and technology in a more socially responsible and environmentally sustainable direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_overpopulation">Reducing overpopulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Reducing overpopulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_population_planning" title="Human population planning">Human population planning</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_empowerment">Education and empowerment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Education and empowerment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One option is to focus on education about overpopulation, <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> methods, and to make birth-control devices like male and <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condoms" title="Condoms">condoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">contraceptive pills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine devices</a> easily available. Worldwide, nearly 40% of <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">pregnancies are unintended</a> (some 80 million unintended pregnancies each year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> An estimated 350 million women in the poorest countries of the world either did not want their last child, do not want another child or want to space their pregnancies, but they lack access to information, affordable means and services to determine the size and spacing of their families.  In the United States, in 2001, almost half of <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">pregnancies were unintended</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>, some 514,000 women die annually of complications from pregnancy and abortion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> with 86% of these deaths occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> region and South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> Additionally, 8 million infants die, many because of <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> or preventable diseases, especially from lack of access to clean drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">Women's rights</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a> in particular are issues regarded to have vital importance in the debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popsearch_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-popsearch-287">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The only ray of hope I can see – and it's not much – is that wherever women are put in control of their lives, both politically and socially, where medical facilities allow them to deal with birth control and where their husbands allow them to make those decisions, birth rate falls. Women don't want to have 12 kids of whom nine will die.
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; line-height:normal; text-align: right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/David_Attenborough" title="David Attenborough">David Attenborough</a>, <i>The Independent</i>, 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> announced a program to reduce its overpopulation by family planning education and putting <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_workforce" title="Women in the workforce">women in the workforce</a>. It was announced in June 2008 by the Minister of Health and Population, and the government has set aside 480 million Egyptian pounds (about $90 million US) for the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several scientists (including e.g. <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul and Anne Ehrlich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gretchen_Daily" title="Gretchen Daily">Gretchen Daily</a>) proposed that humanity should work at stabilizing its absolute numbers, as a starting point towards beginning the process of reducing the total numbers. They suggested the following solutions and policies: following a small-family-size socio-cultural-behavioral norm worldwide (especially one-child-per-family ethos), and providing contraception to all along with proper education on its use and benefits (while providing access to safe, legal abortion as a backup to contraception), combined with a significantly more equitable distribution of resources globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>Business magnate <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a> proposed a "voluntary, non-imposed" one-child-per-family cultural norm.  A "<a href="/wiki/Population_Matters#Pledge_two_or_fewer" title="Population Matters">pledge two or fewer</a>" campaign is run by <a href="/wiki/Population_Matters" title="Population Matters">Population Matters</a> (a UK population concern organisation), in which people are encouraged to limit themselves to small family size.
</p><p>Population planning that is intended to reduce population size or growth rate may promote or enforce one or more of the following practices, although there are other methods as well:
</p>
<ul><li>Greater and better access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraception" title="Contraception">contraception</a></li>
<li>Reducing <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> so that parents do not need to have many children to ensure at least some survive to adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup></li>
<li>Improving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Status_of_women" title="Status of women">status of women</a> in order to facilitate a departure from traditional sexual division of labour.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_child_policy" title="One child policy">One-Child</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_child_policy" title="Two child policy">Two-Child</a> policies, and other policies restricting or discouraging births directly.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryerson_2010_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryerson_2010-293">[293]</a></sup></li>
<li>Creating small family "role models"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryerson_2010_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryerson_2010-293">[293]</a></sup></li>
<li>Tighter immigration restrictions</li></ul>
<p>The method(s) chosen can be strongly influenced by the cultural and religious beliefs of community members.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_regulations">Birth regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Birth regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Overpopulation can be mitigated by <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>; some nations, like the People's Republic of China, use strict measures to reduce birth rates. Religious and ideological opposition to birth control has been cited as a factor contributing to overpopulation and poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sanjay_Gandhi" title="Sanjay Gandhi">Sanjay Gandhi</a>, son of late Prime Minister of India <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi" title="Indira Gandhi">Indira Gandhi</a>, implemented a forced <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilization" title="Compulsory sterilization">sterilization</a> programme between 1975 and 1977. Officially, men with two children or more had to submit to sterilization, but there was a greater focus on sterilizing women than sterilizing men. Some unmarried young men and political opponents may also have been sterilized<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. This program is still remembered and criticized in India, and is blamed for creating a public aversion to <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a>, which hampered government programs for decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>Urban designer <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Arth" title="Michael E. Arth">Michael E. Arth</a> has proposed a "choice-based, marketable birth license plan" he calls "<a href="/wiki/Birth_credit" title="Birth credit">birth credits</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> Birth credits would allow any woman to have as many children as she wants, as long as she buys a license for any children beyond an average allotment that would result in <a href="/wiki/Zero_population_growth" title="Zero population growth">zero population growth</a>. If that allotment was determined to be one child, for example, then the first child would be free, and the market would determine what the license fee for each additional child would cost. Extra credits would expire after a certain time, so these credits could not be hoarded by speculators. The actual cost of the credits would only be a fraction of the actual <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_raising_a_child" title="Cost of raising a child">cost of having and raising a child</a>, so the credits would serve more as a wake-up call to women who might otherwise produce children without seriously considering the long term consequences to themselves or society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362-297">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg"><img alt="Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="604" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg/350px-Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg/525px-Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Growthbydevelopedvslessdeveloped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div><p> Another choice-based approach, similar to Arth's birth credits, is financial compensation or other benefits (free goods and/or services) by the state (or state-owned companies) offered to people who voluntarily undergo <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(medicine)" title="Sterilization (medicine)">sterilization</a>. Such compensation has been offered in the past by the government of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014 the United Nations estimated there is an 80% likelihood that the world's population will be between 9.6 billion and 12.3 billion by 2100. Most of the world's expected population increase will be in Africa and southern Asia.  Africa's population is expected to rise from the current one billion to four billion by 2100, and Asia could add another billion in the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> Because the median age of Africans is relatively low (e.g. in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> it is 15 years old) birth credits would have to limit fertility to one child per two women to reach the levels of developed countries immediately. For countries with a wide base in their <a href="/wiki/Population_pyramid" title="Population pyramid">population pyramid</a> it will take a generation for the people who are of child bearing age to have their families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362-297">[297]</a></sup>  An example of <a href="/wiki/Demographic_momentum" title="Demographic momentum">demographic momentum</a> is China, which added perhaps 400,000 more people after its one-child policy was enacted. Arth has suggested that the focus should be on the developed countries and that some combination of birth credits and additional compensation supplied by the developed countries could rapidly lead to zero population growth while also quickly raising the standard of living in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362_297-2"><a href="#cite_note-Arth,_Michael_E._2010,_pp._352-362-297">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_settlement">Extraterrestrial settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">Space colonization</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Space_advocacy" title="Space advocacy">Space advocacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Pushing_some_of_the_terrestrial_limits_into_outer_space" title="Steady-state economy">Steady-state economy  § Pushing some of the terrestrial limits into outer space</a></div>
<p>Various scientists and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> authors have contemplated that overpopulation on Earth may be remedied in the future by the use of extraterrestrial settlements. In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill" title="Gerard K. O'Neill">Gerard K. O'Neill</a> suggested building <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_habitats" title="Space habitats">space habitats</a> that could support 30,000 times the carrying capacity of Earth using just the asteroid belt, and that the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> as a whole could sustain current population growth rates for a thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Savage" title="Marshall Savage">Marshall Savage</a> (1992, 1994) has projected a human population of five <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1018" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">quintillion</a> (5 x 10<sup>18</sup>)
throughout the Solar System by 3000, with the majority in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Dyson" title="Freeman Dyson">Freeman Dyson</a> (1999) favours the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> as the future home of humanity, suggesting this could happen within a few centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup> In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mining_the_Sky:_Untold_Riches_from_the_Asteroids,_Comets,_and_Planets" title="Mining the Sky: Untold Riches from the Asteroids, Comets, and Planets">Mining the Sky</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/John_S._Lewis" title="John S. Lewis">John S. Lewis</a> suggests that the resources of the solar system could support 10 <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1015" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">quadrillion</a> (10<sup>16</sup>) people. In an interview, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> claimed that overpopulation is a threat to human existence and "our only chance of long-term survival is not to remain inward looking on planet Earth but to spread out into space."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">K. Eric Drexler</a>, famous inventor of the futuristic concept of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">molecular nanotechnology</a>, has suggested in <i><a href="/wiki/Engines_of_Creation" title="Engines of Creation">Engines of Creation</a></i> that colonizing space will mean breaking the <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian limits</a> to growth for the human species.
</p><p>It may be possible for other parts of the Solar System to be inhabited by humanity at some point in the future. <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis" title="Geoffrey A. Landis">Geoffrey Landis</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">Glenn Research Center</a> in particular has pointed out that "[at] cloud-top level, Venus is the paradise planet", as one could construct <a href="/wiki/Aerostat" title="Aerostat">aerostat</a> habitats and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floating_city_(science_fiction)" title="Floating city (science fiction)">floating cities</a> there easily, based on the concept that breathable air is a lifting gas in the dense <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Venusian atmosphere</a>. <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> would, like also <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, in the upper layers of their atmospheres, even afford a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> almost exactly as strong as <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">that on Earth</a> (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Venus" title="Colonization of Venus">colonization of Venus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> authors, including <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clarkebipeds_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-clarkebipeds-305">[305]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> have argued that shipping any excess population into space is not a viable solution to human overpopulation. According to Clarke, "the population battle must be fought or won here on Earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clarkebipeds_305-1"><a href="#cite_note-clarkebipeds-305">[305]</a></sup> The problem for these authors is not the lack of resources in space (as shown in books such as <i>Mining the Sky</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup>), but the physical impracticality of shipping vast numbers of people into space to "solve" overpopulation on Earth. However, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill" title="Gerard K. O'Neill">Gerard K. O'Neill</a>'s calculations show that Earth could offload all new population growth with a launch services industry about the same size as the current airline industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/StarTram" title="StarTram">StarTram</a> concept, by <a href="/wiki/James_R._Powell" title="James R. Powell">James R. Powell</a> (the co-inventor of <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">maglev</a> transport) and others, envisions a capability to send up to 4 million people a decade to space per facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarTram2010_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-StarTram2010-309">[309]</a></sup> A hypothetical extraterrestrial colony could potentially grow by reproduction only (i.e., without any immigration), with all of the inhabitants being the direct descendants of the original colonists.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanization">Urbanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Urbanization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite the increase in population density within cities (and the emergence of megacities), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Habitat" title="UN Habitat">UN Habitat</a> states in its reports that urbanization may be the best compromise in the face of global population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> Cities concentrate human activity within limited areas, limiting the breadth of environmental damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> But this mitigating influence can only be achieved if <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> is significantly improved<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup> and city services are properly maintained.
</p>
